Boris Burkov

Patches Submitted (11)
[PATCH v4 2/4] btrfs: account for compression in delalloc extent reservation Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch addresses an issue in the Btrfs file system where delayed reference reservations were under-estimated due to compression. When compression is enabled, the maximum extent size is reduced from 128MiB to 128KiB, leading to a significant discrepancy between estimated and actual extents. The patch introduces a new function `btrfs_inode_max_extents` to calculate the correct number of extents based on the inode's properties and the maximum extent size. This change ensures that reservations are accurately calculated even when compression is enabled, preventing under-reservation of delayed reference reservations for delalloc writes.

[PATCH v4 3/4] btrfs: make inode->outstanding_extents a u64 Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch changes the type of `outstanding_extents` in the Btrfs inode structure from a 32-bit unsigned integer to a 64-bit unsigned integer (`u64`). This is done because the maximum file size, defined by `MAX_LFS_FILESIZE`, can result in extent sizes that exceed the bounds of a 32-bit unsigned integer. The patch also updates related functions and includes additional assertions to ensure correctness.

[PATCH v4 4/4] btrfs: cap shrink_delalloc iterations to 128M Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch limits the number of iterations in the shrink_delalloc function to prevent it from blocking indefinitely when dealing with large amounts of writeback data. The function now iterates over delalloc work in units of 128MiB, rather than trying to reclaim all available space at once. This change aims to strike a balance between making progress on space reclaim and avoiding arbitrarily long syscall latencies.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-04-06 Last comment 2026-04-07 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v3] btrfs: btrfs_log_dev_io_error() on all bio errors Neutral LLM

This patch modifies the btrfs_log_dev_io_error() function to log device I/O errors for all bio error codes, not just BLK_STS_IOERR and BLK_STS_TARGET. The change is motivated by the observation that some devices return unexpected error codes, leading to incorrect conclusions about system behavior.

Signals: LGTM
Anand Jain Reviewed-by Neutral LLM
Anand points out that BLK_STS_PROTECTION is interpreted differently depending on the device type and suggests that the patch is NVMe-specific.
Show original comment 
On 7/4/26 00:15, Boris Burkov wrote:
> As far as I can tell, we never intentionally constrained ourselves to
> these status codes, and it is misleading and surprising to lack the
> bdev error logging when we get a different error code from the block
> layer. This can lead to jumping to a wrong conclusion like "this
> system didn't see any bio failures but aborted with EIO".
> 

> For example on nvme devices, I observe many failures coming back as
> BLK_STS_MEDIUM. It is apparent that the nvme driver returns a variety of
> BLK_STS_* status values in nvme_error_status().


BLK_STS_PROTECTION is interpreted differently depending on the device.
In NVMe, it indicates invalid Protection Information (PI), while in
HDDs/SSDs it typically points to a malformed CDB (often a software bug).
This is one of several possible error conditions.

Rather than introducing new BLK_STS_* codes, vendors have reused
existing ones for newer device types to maintain backward compatibility.

There isn't much we can do about this for now. I assume the malformed
CDB-related issues have largely been resolved, so this Btrfs fix is
effectively NVMe-specific.

Reviewed-by: Anand Jain <asj@kernel.org>

Thanks
Anand

[PATCH v3 0/4] btrfs: improve stalls under sudden writeback Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series aims to improve stalls under sudden writeback in Btrfs file systems by addressing issues related to delalloc work and metadata space system. The patches introduce targeted improvements, including better reservations for delayed refs and smoothing of the work in flush_space(). This is achieved through four patches: (1) reserving space for delayed refs in delalloc, (2) accounting for compression in delalloc extent reservation, (3) making inode->outstanding_extents a u64, and (4) capping shrink_delalloc iterations. The changes are designed to reduce stalls on systems with large memory and disk sizes, where sudden writeback can cause system-wide issues.

[PATCH v3 1/4] btrfs: reserve space for delayed_refs in delalloc Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch addresses an issue in the Btrfs file system where space is not reserved for delayed references (delayed_refs) during delalloc operations. Delalloc uses a per-inode block reservation to anticipate metadata needs, but it does not account for delayed refs. As a result, when writeback occurs and delayed refs are generated, they draw from an under-reserved trans_handle's delayed refs reservation. This can lead to incorrect assessments of reserved space in the metadata space reclaim logic, causing poor decision making and blocking file system operations.

[PATCH v3 2/4] btrfs: account for compression in delalloc extent reservation Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch addresses an issue in the Btrfs file system where delayed reference reservations were under-estimated due to compression. Specifically, when COMPRESS_FORCE is set or the inode is configured to always compress, the maximum compressed extent size (128KiB) is significantly smaller than the maximum uncompressed extent size (128MiB). The patch introduces a new function `btrfs_inode_max_extents` that takes into account the compression type and returns the correct number of extents for reservation. This change prevents under-reservation of delayed refs reservations for delalloc writes, even when compression is forced. Additionally, the patch removes an unnecessary shim function `count_max_extents` and simplifies the code in the process.

[PATCH v3 3/4] btrfs: make inode->outstanding_extents a u64 Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch updates the data type of 'outstanding_extents' in the Btrfs inode structure from u32 to u64. This change is motivated by the fact that the maximum file size is LLONG_MAX, which results in a computation bound above by ~2^63 / 2^27. With recent changes to divide by 128KiB in compressed cases, this bound is even higher. The patch also updates the 'mod_outstanding_extents' function to handle u64 inputs and adds assertions to ensure that the inputs are valid. This change aims to capture the intent of the types and make the code more robust.

[PATCH v3 4/4] btrfs: cap shrink_delalloc iterations to 128M Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch limits the iterations of the `shrink_delalloc` function in Btrfs to prevent it from blocking indefinitely, especially when dealing with large amounts of writeback data. The function is modified to process delalloc work in units of 128MiB, rather than trying to reclaim large chunks at once. This change aims to strike a balance between making progress on space reclaim and avoiding arbitrarily long syscall latencies and task hangs.

[PATCH v4 0/4] btrfs: improve stalls under sudden writeback Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series aims to reduce stalls caused by sudden writeback on systems with large memory and disk sizes. The patches improve reservations for delayed refs in delalloc work, mirroring inode->block_rsv, and reduce the size of the unit of work in shrink_delalloc(). This is done to prevent a tailspin in metadata space system due to excessive delalloc work and delayed refs blocking all users. The approach taken is relatively targeted, with further work possible to improve flush_space() heuristics and latency.

[PATCH v4 1/4] btrfs: reserve space for delayed_refs in delalloc Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch addresses a problem in the Btrfs file system where space is not reserved for delayed references (delayed_refs) during delalloc operations. Delalloc uses a per-inode block reservation to anticipate metadata needs, but it does not account for delayed refs. This can lead to incorrect assessments of available space and poor decision-making in metadata reclaim logic. The patch fixes this by reserving space for delayed refs in the delalloc process, ensuring that these references are properly accounted for during writeback operations.

Last comment 2026-04-09 Re: [PATCH] btrfs: fix missing last_unlink_trans update when removing a directory Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a bug where the last_unlink_trans update was not being updated when removing a directory in Btrfs. The issue arose from an intentional change to only call btrfs_record_unlink_dir() on success, which could lead to unexpected behavior if btrfs_unlink() does not react correctly to non-zero return values. The patch updates the code to ensure that btrfs_record_unlink_dir() is always called before unlink operations.

🔥 TODAY Re: Simple quota unsafe? RIP: 0010:__btrfs_free_extent.isra.0+0xc41/0x1020 [btrfs] / do_free_extent_accounting:2999: errno=-2 No such entry Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a quota-related issue in the BTRFS file system, specifically an error that occurs when trying to free extents while running delayed refs. The problem arises from an invalid state caused by a previous transaction commit, leading to a '-2 No such entry' error. To fix this, the patch modifies the __btrfs_free_extent function to handle this specific scenario and prevent the quota-related errors. The approach taken involves adding additional checks and handling for the invalid state, ensuring that the BTRFS file system can recover from such situations without crashing or entering a readonly mode.

Reviewer expressed concern about the safety of enabling quotas in Linux kernel version 6.12 due to potential issues causing filesystem corruption and unmountability, specifically mentioning a report from 'moremagic' where the filesystem was unable to be mounted read/write. Reviewer noted that the issue is specific to Raspberry Pi's custom kernel version 6.12 and suggested disabling quotas due to potential instability, as they cannot afford a crash on their large filesystem. Reviewer Marc MERLIN expressed confusion about the patch's story and asked for clarification on how to fix quota problems, but did not raise any specific technical concerns or suggestions. Reviewer noted that switching to 16k native filesystems on arm caused issues with btrfs send/receive, specifically breaking the ability to send from a 4k FS to a 16k FS. Reviewer Marc MERLIN reported crashes on multiple systems after enabling quotas, citing a different path abort in __btrfs_free_extent due to an invalid state, and requested confirmation that quotas are not safe, especially with 16k pages in the kernel but 4k in the filesystem. Reviewer Marc MERLIN expressed confusion about the issue, admitted being clueless about btrfs kernel code, and took a workaround by disabling quotas on all but one system to gather more data for correlation.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-26 Last comment 2026-04-07 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 0/5] locking: contended_release tracepoint instrumentation Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds a new tracepoint called contended_release that fires on the lock holder's side when a lock is released while there are waiters. This provides two main benefits: it allows for hold time estimation by correlating contention_end and contended_release events, and it captures the holder's stack at release time, which can be different from what is captured earlier if the holder does significant work between the waiter's arrival and unlock. The patch also fixes several issues in the previous versions, including spurious events, use-after-free errors, and build failures on certain architectures.

★ Maintainer review: Matthew Wilcox, Steven Rostedt
10 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Matthew Wilcox ★ Maintainer ↳ replying to Dmitry Ilvokhin Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Matthew Wilcox questioned the usefulness of the contended_release tracepoint, specifically asking for explanations on how it provides value in measuring hold time and capturing the holder's stack at release time.
Show original comment 
As someone who's not an expert in this area (so please use short words
to explain it to me), why do we want to know how long this holder took
to release the lock from when it became contended?

I understand why we want to know how long any given waiter had to wait
to gain the lock (but we already have tracepoints which show that).

I also don't understand why we want to know the holder's stack at
release time.  The stack at contention-begin time will include
the point at which the lock was acquired which should be correlated
with where the lock was released.

Perhaps examples might help me understand why we want this?

Steven Rostedt ★ Maintainer ↳ replying to Matthew Wilcox Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Steven Rostedt noted that the tracepoint placement for queued spinlocks and rwlocks should fire before the lock is released, not after, to accurately capture the holder's stack at release time. The reviewer, Steven Rostedt, pointed out that the contended_release tracepoint would be useful for determining how long a critical section is held when a lock is highly contended by many tasks. He suggested that seeing the release time and location would help identify if the length of the critical section is causing the contention.
Show original comment 
On Thu, 26 Mar 2026 15:55:21 +0000
Matthew Wilcox <willy@infradead.org> wrote:

---

Dmitry could give his own rationale for this, but I have my only use case.

This would be useful to find out how long the critical section is. If a
lock is highly contended by many tasks, you could get a high contention
time simply because other tasks are causing the delay for the waiter.
Seeing the release time and location would let you also know how long the
critical section was held, and if the length of the critical section is
causing the contention.

Having a stack trace of the release would differentiate the path that
released the lock, as there can be many places that release them. Although,
I have to admit, I'm not sure there are many different places locks are
released. Especially now that we have guard(), which will make all the
releases in a function at the same location.

-- Steve

Signals: requested changes, requested additional information, suggested use case
Dmitry Ilvokhin (author) ↳ replying to Matthew Wilcox Neutral LLM
The author is addressing Matthew Wilcox's suggestion to come up with generic instrumentation for contended release tracepoint, explaining that the current implementation provides a cause (holder time) and symptom (waiter time), but requires different treatments depending on the lock usage pattern. The author addressed Matthew Wilcox's concern that the --lock-owner feature does not work for all lock types by explaining that contended_release tracepoint fires in the holder's context, providing the holder's stack at release time, which is necessary to identify the holders and what they were doing.
Show original comment 
I think the simplest way to think about it is the following. Waiter time
is the symptom, while holder time is the cause.

The waiter-side contention_begin/contention_end tells us how long a
waiter waited, but that time can span multiple holders.

If a waiter waited 10 ms, we can not tell whether one holder held the
lock for 10 ms or five holders held it for 2 ms each. These need
different treatments: the first means shrink the critical section, the
second means reduce lock frequency or split the lock. Today we can not
distinguish between these cases from waiter-side data alone.

---

Holder's stack allows us to understand who exactly waiters were waiting
for to release the lock.

The stack at contention_begin time does not always include the holder's
stack. The --lock-owner feature works by reading the owner field from
the lock struct, but it only supports mutex and rwsem. For spinlocks,
queued rwlocks, semaphores, and several others, the waiter has no
visibility into the holder whatsoever.

contended_release fires in the holder's context, so we get the holder's
stack at release time. For spinlocks, this is the only way to get any
holder-side information.

Original motivation was zone lock contention (a spinlock) in Meta
production workloads. We could see waiters were blocked, but had no way
to identify the holders or what they were doing.
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation, acknowledged

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-26 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH v4 2/5] locking/percpu-rwsem: Extract __percpu_up_read() Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch extracts the slowpath of percpu_up_read() into a new function __percpu_up_read(). The original percpu_up_read() is simplified and no longer contains the slowpath, which is moved to the new function. This change avoids increasing binary size due to the addition of a tracepoint to an inlined function. The patch also adds an export for the new function.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-26 Last comment 2026-03-31 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 3/5] locking: Add contended_release tracepoint to sleepable locks Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a new tracepoint called contended_release to the Linux kernel's locking subsystem. The contended_release tracepoint fires on the holder side when a contended lock is released, allowing for correlation of lock hold time under contention with waiter events by lock address. The patch modifies the slowpath unlock paths of various sleepable locks (mutex, rtmutex, semaphore, rwsem, percpu-rwsem, and RT-specific rwbase locks) to fire the tracepoint before releasing the lock or waking the waiter. This enables more detailed tracing and debugging of locking behavior in the kernel.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-26 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH v4 4/5] locking: Factor out queued_spin_release() Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch introduces a new function called queued_spin_release(), which is an arch-overridable unlock primitive. It renames existing arch-specific queued_spin_unlock() overrides on x86 (paravirt) and MIPS to queued_spin_release(). The change is preparatory for the next commit, which will add contended release tracepoint instrumentation to queued_spin_unlock(). No functional changes are made.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-26 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH v4 5/5] locking: Add contended_release tracepoint to spinning locks Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a contended_release tracepoint to queued spinlocks and rwlocks, allowing for tracing of lock contention events. The patch uses an arch-overridable function to ensure correct behavior across all architectures, including those with custom unlock implementations. When the tracepoint is disabled, it has minimal impact on performance, adding only a single NOP instruction to the hot path. However, when enabled, it can increase binary size by up to 0.24% due to the need to preserve the lock pointer in a callee-saved register across the trace call.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-04-06 Last comment 2026-04-07 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH] dcache: warn when a dentry is freed with a non-empty ->d_lru Neutral LLM

This patch adds a WARN_ON_ONCE() in the dentry_free() function to warn when a dentry is freed with a non-empty ->d_lru list. The warning is intended to detect and prevent use-after-free (UAF) bugs, which have been observed in certain scenarios.

★ Maintainer review: Jan Kara
1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Jan Kara ★ Maintainer ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Reviewed-by Positive LLM

Last comment 2026-04-09 [PATCH v2 0/3] mm: improve write performance with RWF_DONTCACHE
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] mm: kick writeback flusher instead of inline flush for IOCB_DONTCACHE Neutral Last comment 2026-04-09 Ritesh (IBM) Christoph Hellwig
  • [2/3] testing: add nfsd-io-bench NFS server benchmark suite
  • [3/3] testing: add dontcache-bench local filesystem benchmark suite
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch set improves write performance when using the RWF_DONTCACHE flag by kicking the flusher thread for the superblock instead of initiating writeback directly. This change is based on benchmarking results that show a clear improvement over the status quo, particularly in cases with heavy write activity. The patch also includes a comparison of different IO modes for nfsd and highlights the poor performance of RWF_DONTCACHE with write-heavy workloads. The approach taken involves modifying the generic_write_sync function to kick the flusher thread, which is expected to better isolate the DONTCACHE writeback behavior and improve overall performance.

★ Maintainer review: Jeff Layton (author)
6 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Jeff Layton (author) ★ Maintainer Needs Work LLM
The author is addressing concerns about the performance of the per-inode writeback approach, specifically that it regresses on multi-writer and random write workloads due to lost I/O batching. The author acknowledges this regression and suggests sticking with v2, which has a different approach.
Show original comment 
Actually, that version regressed performance in a couple of cases. I think v2 is probably the best approach, on balance. Maybe we can get this into -next so that it can make v7.2?

Here's the comparison between this version and a writeback_single_inode() flush version:

------------------8<-----------------------

● Comparing dontcache numbers against the previous whole-BDI flusher kernel (from /tmp/dontcache-local-4way-flusher.md):                                                                          

  Per-Inode vs Whole-BDI Flusher — DONTCACHE on Local XFS                                                                                                                                         

  Single-Client Writes

  ┌──────────────────┬───────────┬───────────┬─────────────┐                                                                                                                                      
  │    Benchmark     │ Whole-BDI │ Per-Inode │   Change    │                                                                                                                                      
  ├──────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤      
  │ Seq write MB/s   │ 1450      │ 1438      │ -1% (noise) │
  ├──────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Seq write p99.9  │ 23.5 ms   │ 23.5 ms   │ identical   │
  ├──────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤                                                                                                                                      
  │ Rand write MB/s  │ 363       │ 286       │ -21%        │
  ├──────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤                                                                                                                                      
  │ Rand write p99.9 │ 1.8 ms    │ 16.7 ms   │ regression  │
  └──────────────────┴───────────┴───────────┴─────────────┘

  Seq write is identical. Rand write regressed — the whole-BDI flusher batched all dirty pages in one pass with writeback_sb_inodes() under a single blk_plug, while per-inode write_inode_now() loses that batching.

  Single-Client Reads

  ┌────────────────┬───────────┬───────────┬────────┐
  │   Benchmark    │ Whole-BDI │ Per-Inode │ Change │
  ├────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ Seq read MB/s  │ 2950      │ 2350      │ -20%   │
  ├────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ Rand read MB/s │ 651       │ 519       │ -20%   │
  └────────────────┴───────────┴───────────┴────────┘

  Reads shouldn't be affected by writeback path changes. Buffered reads also dropped (2888 → 2331), suggesting different system conditions between runs rather than a per-inode regression.

  Multi-Writer (Scenario A)

  ┌────────────────┬───────────┬───────────┬────────────┐
  │     Metric     │ Whole-BDI │ Per-Inode │   Change   │
  ├────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼────────────┤
  │ Aggregate MB/s │ 1478      │ 999       │ -32%       │
  ├────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼────────────┤
  │ p99.9          │ 46 ms     │ 77 ms     │ -67% worse │
  └────────────────┴───────────┴───────────┴────────────┘

  This is the biggest regression. With whole-BDI, the flusher did one batched pass through all dirty inodes via writeback_sb_inodes(). With per-inode, each of 4 writers queues a separate work item processed serially by write_inode_now() — losing the batch I/O merging benefit.

  Scenario C & D (Noisy Neighbor)

  ┌─────────────────────────┬───────────┬───────────┬─────────────┐
  │         Metric          │ Whole-BDI │ Per-Inode │   Change    │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Scenario C writer       │ 1468      │ 1386      │ -6%         │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Scenario C readers      │ 18.7 MB/s │ 18.7 MB/s │ identical   │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Scenario D writer       │ 1472      │ 1467      │ identical   │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Scenario D readers      │ 496 MB/s  │ 507 MB/s  │ +2%         │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────┼─────────────┤
  │ Scenario D reader p99.9 │ 440 us    │ 358 us    │ +19% better │
  └─────────────────────────┴───────────┴───────────┴─────────────┘

  Mixed-mode (Scenario D) is the intended production case and it's essentially identical or slightly better — per-inode writeback creates less device contention for buffered readers.

  Summary

  The per-inode approach is neutral-to-slightly-better for the production scenario (Scenario D), but regresses on multi-writer and random write workloads. The core issue is loss of I/O batching
 — writeback_sb_inodes() processes all dirty inodes in one blk_plug'd pass, while per-inode write_inode_now() calls are processed one at a time. The read regressions likely reflect different
  system conditions since buffered/direct reads also dropped ~20%.

-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

Ritesh (IBM) Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Ritesh pointed out that the current API gives a misleading impression of kicking writeback for only an inode's address space mapping, when in fact it starts writeback for all dirty pages on the respective bdi. He suggested exporting the symbol for wakeup_flusher_threads_bdi() and using it instead, or alternatively modifying filemap_kick_writeback_all(mapping, enum wb_reason) to better reflect its behavior.
Show original comment 
This api gives a wrong sense that we are kicking writeback to write
dirty pages which belongs to only this inode's address space mapping.
But instead we are starting wb for everything on the respective bdi.

So instead why not just export symbol for wakeup_flusher_threads_bdi()
and use it instead?

If not, then IMO at least making it... 
   filemap_kick_writeback_all(mapping, enum wb_reason)

... might be better.

Signals: requested changes
Christoph Hellwig Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Christoph Hellwig noted that the current implementation of wakeup_flusher_threads_bdi causes WB_SYNC_NONE writeback to occur every time a DONTCACHE write finishes, which is inefficient and unnecessary. He suggested introducing a new WB_start_dontcache bit, a get_nr_dontcache_pages() helper function, and other related changes.
Show original comment 
wakeup_flusher_threads_bdi ends up calling wb_start_writeback eventually,
which sets WB_start_all, pushes the reason to start_all_reason and then
does the actual wakeup.

The flusher thread then through wb_check_start_all does a WB_SYNC_NONE
writeback based on get_nr_dirty_pages.  Which seems wrong - we don't
want to do a huge writeback evertime the some DONTCACHE write finished.

So I think you'll want a new WB_start_dontcache bit, a new
get_nr_dontcache_pages() helper on a new node counter, etc.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-03-24 Last comment 2026-04-07 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v2 0/2] nfs: delegated attribute fixes
Show 2 individual patches
  • [1/2] nfs: fix utimensat() for atime with delegated timestamps Neutral Submitted 2026-03-24Last comment 2026-04-07 ★ Jeff Layton (author) Olga Kornievskaia
  • [2/2] nfs: update inode ctime after removexattr operation Neutral Submitted 2026-03-24Last comment 2026-03-31 Olga Kornievskaia ★ Jeff Layton (author) Thomas Haynes Rick Macklem
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch set addresses issues related to delegated attributes in the NFS file system, specifically fixing problems with utimensat() and removexattr operations. The patches update inode timestamps and ctime after attribute changes, ensuring accurate and consistent behavior. The fixes are based on a previous version of the patch set, which has been updated with Fixes: tags for better tracking.

★ Maintainer review: Jeff Layton (author)
13 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Jeff Layton (author) ★ Maintainer Neutral LLM
Author addressed a concern about the proposed fix for xfstest generic/728 failing with delegated timestamps. The author acknowledged that the server's mtime and ctime are up-to-date after a REMOVEXATTR operation, making an additional SETATTR unnecessary. They explained their approach as relying on the fact that the server updates change_attr on operations that might not necessarily happen.
Show original comment 
On Tue, 2026-03-24 at 13:32 -0400, Jeff Layton wrote:
> xfstest generic/221 is failing with delegated timestamps enabled.  When
> the client holds a WRITE_ATTRS_DELEG delegation, and a userland process
> does a utimensat() for only the atime, the ctime is not properly
> updated. The problem is that the client tries to cache the atime update,
> but there is no mtime update, so the delegated attribute update never
> updates the ctime.
> 
> Delegated timestamps don't have a mechanism to update the ctime in
> accordance with atime-only changes due to utimensat() and the like.
> Change the client to issue an RPC in this case, so that the ctime gets
> properly updated alongside the atime.
> 
> Fixes: 40f45ab3814f ("NFS: Further fixes to attribute delegation a/mtime changes")
> Reported-by: Olga Kornievskaia <aglo@umich.edu>
> Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
> ---
>  fs/nfs/inode.c | 9 +--------
>  1 file changed, 1 insertion(+), 8 deletions(-)
> 
> diff --git a/fs/nfs/inode.c b/fs/nfs/inode.c
> index 4786343eeee0f874aa1f31ace2f35fdcb83fc7a6..3a5bba7e3c92d4d4fcd65234cd2f10e56f78dee0 100644
> --- a/fs/nfs/inode.c
> +++ b/fs/nfs/inode.c
> @@ -757,14 +757,7 @@ nfs_setattr(struct mnt_idmap *idmap, struct dentry *dentry,
>  	} else if (nfs_have_delegated_atime(inode) &&
>  		   attr->ia_valid & ATTR_ATIME &&
>  		   !(attr->ia_valid & ATTR_MTIME)) {
> -		if (attr->ia_valid & ATTR_ATIME_SET) {
> -			if (uid_eq(task_uid, owner_uid)) {
> -				spin_lock(&inode->i_lock);
> -				nfs_set_timestamps_to_ts(inode, attr);
> -				spin_unlock(&inode->i_lock);
> -				attr->ia_valid &= ~(ATTR_ATIME|ATTR_ATIME_SET);
> -			}
> -		} else {

This probably deserves a comment. How about something like:

		/*
		 * An atime-only update via an explicit setattr (e.g.: utimensat() and
		 * the like) requires updating the ctime as well. Delegated timestamps
		 * don't have a mechanism for updating the ctime with a delegated
		 * atime-only update, so an RPC must be issued.
		 */

> +		if (!(attr->ia_valid & ATTR_ATIME_SET)) {
>  			nfs_update_delegated_atime(inode);
>  			attr->ia_valid &= ~ATTR_ATIME;
>  		}

-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

---

On Fri, 2026-03-27 at 11:11 -0400, Olga Kornievskaia wrote:
> On Tue, Mar 24, 2026 at 1:32 PM Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org> wrote:
> > 
> > xfstest generic/728 fails with delegated timestamps. The client does a
> > removexattr and then a stat to test the ctime, which doesn't change. The
> > stat() doesn't trigger a GETATTR because of the delegated timestamps, so
> > it relies on the cached ctime, which is wrong.
> > 
> > The setxattr compound has a trailing GETATTR, which ensures that its
> > ctime gets updated. Follow the same strategy with removexattr.
> 
> This approach relies on the fact that the server the serves delegated
> attributes would update change_attr on operations which might now
> necessarily happen (ie, linux server does not update change_attribute
> on writes or clone). I propose an alternative fix for the failing
> generic/728.
> 
> diff --git a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> index 7b3ca68fb4bb..ede1835a45b3 100644
> --- a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> +++ b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> @@ -1389,7 +1389,13 @@ static int _nfs42_proc_removexattr(struct inode
> *inode, const char *name)
>             &res.seq_res, 1);
>         trace_nfs4_removexattr(inode, name, ret);
>         if (!ret)
> -               nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> +               if (nfs_have_delegated_attributes(inode)) {
> +                       nfs_update_delegated_mtime(inode);
> +                       spin_lock(&inode->i_lock);
> +                       nfs_set_cache_invalid(inode, NFS_INO_INVALID_BLOCKS);
> +                       spin_unlock(&inode->i_lock);
> +               } else
> +                       nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> 
>         return ret;
>  }
> 

What's the advantage of doing it this way?

You just sent a REMOVEXATTR operation to the server that will change
the mtime there. The server has the most up-to-date version of the
mtime and ctime at that point.

It's certainly possible that the REMOVEXATTR is the only change that
occurred. With what I'm proposing, we don't even need to do a SETATTR
at all if nothing else changed. With your version, you would.

> > 
> > Fixes: 3e1f02123fba ("NFSv4.2: add client side XDR handling for extended attributes")
> > Reported-by: Olga Kornievskaia <aglo@umich.edu>
> > Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
> > ---
> >  fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c      | 18 ++++++++++++++++--
> >  fs/nfs/nfs42xdr.c       | 10 ++++++++--
> >  include/linux/nfs_xdr.h |  3 +++
> >  3 files changed, 27 insertions(+), 4 deletions(-)
> >

(cc'ing Tom)

That is a very good point.

My interpretation was that delegated timestamps generally covered
writes, but SETATTR style operations that do anything beyond only
changing the mtime can't be cached.

We probably need some delstid spec clarification: for what operations
is the server required to disable timestamp updates when a write
delegation is outstanding?

In the case of nfsd, we disable timestamp updates for WRITE/COPY/CLONE
but not SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.

How does the Hammerspace anvil behave? Does it disable c/mtime updates
for writes when there is an outstanding timestamp delegation like we're
doing in nfsd? If so, does it do the same for
SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR operations as well?

View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged alternative fix, explained reasoning
Jeff Layton (author) ★ Maintainer Needs Work LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the patch's reliance on server behavior for delegated timestamps, specifically whether the server should update ctime/mtime on operations like REMOVEXATTR. The author agrees that there may be inconsistencies in server implementations and suggests that the patch relies too heavily on specific server behavior. They propose looking at how size is handled under delegation in RFC8881 as a model for timestamp handling.

> 
> Jeff,
> 
> I think the right way to look at this is closer to how size is
> handled under delegation in RFC8881, rather than as a per-op rule.
> 
> In our implementation, because we are acting as an MDS and data I/O
> goes to DSes, we already treat size as effectively delegated when
> a write layout is outstanding. The MDS does not maintain authoritative
> size locally in that case. We may refresh size/timestamps internally
> (e.g., on GETATTR by querying DSes), but we don’t treat that as
> overriding the delegated authority.
> 
> For timestamps, our behavior is effectively the same model. When
> the client holds the relevant delegation, the server does not
> consider itself authoritative for ctime/mtime. If current values
> are needed, we can obtain them from the client (e.g., via CB_GETATTR),
> and the client must present the delegation stateid to demonstrate
> that authority. So the authority follows the delegation, not the
> specific operation.
> 
> That said, I don’t think we’ve fully resolved the semantics for all
> metadata-style ops either. WRITE and SETATTR are clear in our model,
> but for things like CLONE/COPY/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR, we’ve likely
> been relying on assumptions rather than a fully consistent rule.
> I.e., CLONE and COPY we just pass through to the DS and we don't
> implement SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> 
> So the spec question, as I see it, is not whether REMOVEXATTR (or
> any particular op) should update ctime/mtime, but whether delegated
> timestamps are meant to follow the same attribute-authority model
> as delegated size in RFC8881. If so, then we expect that the server
> should query the client via CB_GETATTR to return updated ctime/mtime
> after such operations while the delegation is outstanding.
> 

The dilemma we have is: because we _do_ allow local processes to stat()
files that have an outstanding write delegation, we can never allow the
ctime in particular to roll backward (modulo clock jumps).

If we're dealing with changes that have been cached in the client and
are being lazily flushed out, then we can't update the timestamp when
that operation occurs. The time of the RPC to flush the changes will
almost certainly be later than the cached timestamps on the client that
will eventually be set, so when the client comes back we'd end up
violating the rollback rule.

Our only option is to freeze timestamp updates on anything that might
represent such an operation. So far, we only do that on WRITE and COPY
operations -- in general, operations that require an open file, since
FMODE_NOCMTIME is attached to the file.

Some SETATTRs that only update the mtime and atime can be cached on the
client by virtue of the fact that it's authoritative for timestamps.
There are some exceptions though:

- atime-only updates can't be cached since the ctime won't change with
a timestamp update if the mtime didn't change

- if you set the mtime to a time that is later than the time you got
the delegation from the server, but earlier than the current time, you
can't cache that. The ctime would be later than the mtime in that case,
and we don't have a mechanism to handle that in a delegated timestamp
SETATTR.

I don't see how you could reasonably buffer a SETXATTR or REMOVEXATTR
operation to be sent later. These need to be done synchronously since
they could always fail for some reason and we don't have a mechanism at
the syscall layer to handle a deferred error. They also only update the
ctime and not the mtime, and we have no mechanism to do that with
delegated timestamps.

Based on that, I think the client and server both need to ignore the
timestamp delegation on a SETXATTR or REMOVEXATTR. The server should
update the ctime and the client needs to send a trailing GETATTR on the
REMOVEXATTR compound in order to get it and the change attr.

Exactly what this patch does, fwiw...
-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

> > > > > > On Fri, 2026-03-27 at 11:11 -0400, Olga Kornievskaia wrote:
> > > > > > > On Tue, Mar 24, 2026 at 1:32 PM Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org> wrote:
> > > > > > > > 
> > > > > > > > xfstest generic/728 fails with delegated timestamps. The client does a
> > > > > > > > removexattr and then a stat to test the ctime, which doesn't change. The
> > > > > > > > stat() doesn't trigger a GETATTR because of the delegated timestamps, so
> > > > > > > > it relies on the cached ctime, which is wrong.
> > > > > > > > 
> > > > > > > > The setxattr compound has a trailing GETATTR, which ensures that its
> > > > > > > > ctime gets updated. Follow the same strategy with removexattr.
> > > > > > > 
> > > > > > > This approach relies on the fact that the server the serves delegated
> > > > > > > attributes would update change_attr on operations which might now
> > > > > > > necessarily happen (ie, linux server does not update change_attribute
> > > > > > > on writes or clone). I propose an alternative fix for the failing
> > > > > > > generic/728.
> > > > > > > 
> > > > > > > diff --git a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > index 7b3ca68fb4bb..ede1835a45b3 100644
> > > > > > > --- a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > +++ b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > @@ -1389,7 +1389,13 @@ static int _nfs42_proc_removexattr(struct inode
> > > > > > > *inode, const char *name)
> > > > > > >             &res.seq_res, 1);
> > > > > > >         trace_nfs4_removexattr(inode, name, ret);
> > > > > > >         if (!ret)
> > > > > > > -               nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> > > > > > > +               if (nfs_have_delegated_attributes(inode)) {
> > > > > > > +                       nfs_update_delegated_mtime(inode);
> > > > > > > +                       spin_lock(&inode->i_lock);
> > > > > > > +                       nfs_set_cache_invalid(inode, NFS_INO_INVALID_BLOCKS);
> > > > > > > +                       spin_unlock(&inode->i_lock);
> > > > > > > +               } else
> > > > > > > +                       nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> > > > > > > 
> > > > > > >         return ret;
> > > > > > >  }
> > > > > > > 
> > > > > > 
> > > > > > What's the advantage of doing it this way?
> > > > > > 
> > > > > > You just sent a REMOVEXATTR operation to the server that will change
> > > > > > the mtime there. The server has the most up-to-date version of the
> > > > > > mtime and ctime at that point.
> > > > > 
> > > > > In presence of delegated attributes, Is the server required to update
> > > > > its mtime/ctime on an operation? As I mentioned, the linux server does
> > > > > not update its ctime/mtime for WRITE, CLONE, COPY.
> > > > > 
> > > > > Is possible that
> > > > > some implementations might be different and also do not update the
> > > > > ctime/mtime on REMOVEXATTR?
> > > > > 
> > > > > Therefore I was suggesting that the patch
> > > > > relies on the fact that it would receive an updated value. Of course
> > > > > perhaps all implementations are done the same as the linux server and
> > > > > my point is moot. I didn't see anything in the spec that clarifies
> > > > > what the server supposed to do (and client rely on).
> > > > > 
> > > > 
> > > > (cc'ing Tom)
> > > > 
> > > > That is a very good point.
> > > > 
> > > > My interpretation was that delegated timestamps generally covered
> > > > writes, but SETATTR style operations that do anything beyond only
> > > > changing the mtime can't be cached.
> > > > 
> > > > We probably need some delstid spec clarification: for what operations
> > > > is the server required to disable timestamp updates when a write
> > > > delegation is outstanding?
> > > > 
> > > > In the case of nfsd, we disable timestamp updates for WRITE/COPY/CLONE
> > > > but not SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> > > > 
> > > > How does the Hammerspace anvil behave? Does it disable c/mtime updates
> > > > for writes when there is an outstanding timestamp delegation like we're
> > > > doing in nfsd? If so, does it do the same for
> > > > SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR operations as well?
> > > 
> > > Jeff,
> > > 
> > > I think the right way to look at this is closer to how size is
> > > handled under delegation in RFC8881, rather than as a per-op rule.
> > > 
> > > In our implementation, because we are acting as an MDS and data I/O
> > > goes to DSes, we already treat size as effectively delegated when
> > > a write layout is outstanding. The MDS does not maintain authoritative
> > > size locally in that case. We may refresh size/timestamps internally
> > > (e.g., on GETATTR by querying DSes), but we don’t treat that as
> > > overriding the delegated authority.
> > > 
> > > For timestamps, our behavior is effectively the same model. When
> > > the client holds the relevant delegation, the server does not
> > > consider itself authoritative for ctime/mtime. If current values
> > > are needed, we can obtain them from the client (e.g., via CB_GETATTR),
> > > and the client must present the delegation stateid to demonstrate
> > > that authority. So the authority follows the delegation, not the
> > > specific operation.
> > > 
> > > That said, I don’t think we’ve fully resolved the semantics for all
> > > metadata-style ops either. WRITE and SETATTR are clear in our model,
> > > but for things like CLONE/COPY/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR, we’ve likely
> > > been relying on assumptions rather than a fully consistent rule.
> > > I.e., CLONE and COPY we just pass through to the DS and we don't
> > > implement SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> > > 
> > > So the spec question, as I see it, is not whether REMOVEXATTR (or
> > > any particular op) should update ctime/mtime, but whether delegated
> > > timestamps are meant to follow the same attribute-authority model
> > > as delegated size in RFC8881. If so, then we expect that the server
> > > should query the client via CB_GETATTR to return updated ctime/mtime
> > > after such operations while the delegation is outstanding.
> > > 
> > 
> > The dilemma we have is: because we _do_ allow local processes to stat()
> > files that have an outstanding write delegation, we can never allow the
> > ctime in particular to roll backward (modulo clock jumps).
> 
> I agree we do not want visible ctime rollback, but I do not see how
> that can be guaranteed from delegated timestamps alone when the
> authoritative timestamp may be generated on a different node with
> a different clock and the object may change during the CB_GETATTR
> window. That seems to require either monotonic clamping of exposed
> ctime, or treating change_attr rather than ctime as the real
> serialization signal.
> 
> > 
> > If we're dealing with changes that have been cached in the client and
> > are being lazily flushed out, then we can't update the timestamp when
> > that operation occurs. The time of the RPC to flush the changes will
> > almost certainly be later than the cached timestamps on the client that
> > will eventually be set, so when the client comes back we'd end up
> > violating the rollback rule.
> > 
> > Our only option is to freeze timestamp updates on anything that might
> > represent such an operation. So far, we only do that on WRITE and COPY
> > operations -- in general, operations that require an open file, since
> > FMODE_NOCMTIME is attached to the file.
> > 
> > Some SETATTRs that only update the mtime and atime can be cached on the
> > client by virtue of the fact that it's authoritative for timestamps.
> > There are some exceptions though:
> > 
> > - atime-only updates can't be cached since the ctime won't change with
> > a timestamp update if the mtime didn't change
> > 
> > - if you set the mtime to a time that is later than the time you got
> > the delegation from the server, but earlier than the current time, you
> > can't cache that. The ctime would be later than the mtime in that case,
> > and we don't have a mechanism to handle that in a delegated timestamp
> > SETATTR.
> > 
> > I don't see how you could reasonably buffer a SETXATTR or REMOVEXATTR
> > operation to be sent later. These need to be done synchronously since
> > they could always fail for some reason and we don't have a mechanism at
> > the syscall layer to handle a deferred error. They also only update the
> > ctime and not the mtime, and we have no mechanism to do that with
> > delegated timestamps.
> > 
> > Based on that, I think the client and server both need to ignore the
> > timestamp delegation on a SETXATTR or REMOVEXATTR. The server should
> > update the ctime and the client needs to send a trailing GETATTR on the
> > REMOVEXATTR compound in order to get it and the change attr.
> 
> One concern I have with a per-op formulation is extensibility. If
> delegated timestamp behavior is defined by enumerating specific
> operations, then every new operation added to the protocol creates
> a fresh ambiguity until the spec is updated again. It seems better
> to define the behavior in terms of operation properties - e.g., whether
> the operation is synchronously visible, can be deferred/cached at
> the client, and whether it affects only ctime versus mtime/atime -
> so future operations can be classified without reopening the base
> rule.
> 
> I.e., I can't tell if you want me to update the spec with
> guidance per-op or you are just documenting what you did.
> 
> 

I think we probably need some guidance in the spec, and I think that
guidance comes down to: operations that don't have a way to report a
delayed error condition can't be buffered on the client and must
continue to be done synchronously even if a delegation is held.

By way of example: if I do a write() on the client I can buffer that
because userland can eventually do an fsync() to see if it succeeded.
This is not true for syscalls like setxattr() or removexattr(), or most
syscalls that result in a SETATTR operation (chmod(), chown(), etc).

They must be done synchronously because:

1/ there's no way to update only the ctime in a delegated timestamp
update

...and...

2/ these syscalls can fail, so we can't return from them until we know
the outcome.

How best to phrase this guidance, I'm not sure...
-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledges potential fix needed, suggests alternative approach
Jeff Layton (author) ★ Maintainer Needs Work LLM
The author is addressing a concern about delegated timestamps and their impact on server-side updates of mtime and ctime. The reviewer pointed out that the patch relies on the server updating its mtime/ctime after receiving a REMOVEXATTR operation, but noted that this might not be true for all implementations (e.g., Hammerspace anvil). The author agrees with the reviewer's point and suggests that there is a need for clarification in the specification regarding what operations require disabling timestamp updates when a write delegation is outstanding. Specifically, they mention that nfsd disables timestamp updates for WRITE/COPY/CLONE but not SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.

We basically disable c/mtime updates on the delegation when it's handed
out and record the current time (A). We then clamp the delegated
timestamp update to between A and "now" when the SETATTR is done.
That's enough to ensure that nothing rolls backward but we can still
respect the client's update assuming the clocks are close enough.

> > > > 
> > > > If we're dealing with changes that have been cached in the client and
> > > > are being lazily flushed out, then we can't update the timestamp when
> > > > that operation occurs. The time of the RPC to flush the changes will
> > > > almost certainly be later than the cached timestamps on the client that
> > > > will eventually be set, so when the client comes back we'd end up
> > > > violating the rollback rule.
> > > > 
> > > > Our only option is to freeze timestamp updates on anything that might
> > > > represent such an operation. So far, we only do that on WRITE and COPY
> > > > operations -- in general, operations that require an open file, since
> > > > FMODE_NOCMTIME is attached to the file.

> > > > client by virtue of the fact that it's authoritative for timestamps.
> > > > There are some exceptions though:
> > > >

> > > > a timestamp update if the mtime didn't change

> > > > the delegation from the server, but earlier than the current time, you
> > > > can't cache that. The ctime would be later than the mtime in that case,
> > > > and we don't have a mechanism to handle that in a delegated timestamp
> > > > SETATTR.

> > > > they could always fail for some reason and we don't have a mechanism at
> > > > the syscall layer to handle a deferred error. They also only update the
> > > > ctime and not the mtime, and we have no mechanism to do that with
> > > > delegated timestamps.
> > > > 
> > > > Based on that, I think the client and server both need to ignore the
> > > > timestamp delegation on a SETXATTR or REMOVEXATTR. The server should
> > > > update the ctime and the client needs to send a trailing GETATTR on the

> > Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
> 
> One concern I have with the "ops that can report delayed error"
> formulation is that, in practice, any NFSv4 operation may encounter
> a temporary condition and return NFS4ERR_DELAY at execution time.
> Even a simple operation like GETFH may need to allocate reply
> resources and fail transiently. As such, the ability to convey delay
> is not a distinguishing property of a particular operation.
> 
> I think the relevant distinction is instead whether the client may
> complete the syscall locally and defer authoritative execution
> and/or result until a later point without violating its semantics.
> Some operations fit that model, but others do not.
> 
> Even WRITE is not inherently in the "may be deferred" category;
> that depends on the data path. For example, in a pNFS configuration
> where WRITE is routed synchronously to an MDS, completion semantics
> differ from a buffered writeback model. This suggests that the
> classification should not be tied to specific opcodes, but to the
> completion semantics of the operation in the context in which it
> is executed.
> 

Yeah, I phrased that poorly. I didn't mean something that would return
NFS4ERR_DELAY, but rather operations like unstable WRITEs and
asynchronous COPY operations (and anything else that falls into that
model) that can fail after the server has returned from the initial
operation.

> Given that, it may be more useful for the spec to describe delegated
> timestamp behavior in terms of these semantics:
> 
>    o Operations for which the client may lawfully acknowledge
>    completion before authoritative server execution has completed
>    may use delegated timestamp authority, subject to the constraints
>    of that deferred-completion model.
> 
>    o Operations that require authoritative execution status before
>    completion of the user-visible request must be executed synchronously
>    and must not rely on deferred timestamp resolution.
> 
> This avoids per-op enumeration and allows future operations to be
> classified based on their semantics rather than requiring explicit
> specification updates.

Ack. Sounds good to me.

Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

Signals: acknowledgment of need for clarification, agreement with reviewer's point
Olga Kornievskaia Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Olga Kornievskaia proposed an alternative fix for the generic/728 test failure, noting that the original approach relies on server behavior that may not always occur (e.g., updating change_attribute on writes or clone operations). She suggested modifying the nfs42_proc_removexattr function to update the delegated mtime and invalidate the inode's cache when dealing with delegated attributes.

On Tue, Mar 24, 2026 at 1:32 PM Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org> wrote:

> The setxattr compound has a trailing GETATTR, which ensures that its
> ctime gets updated. Follow the same strategy with removexattr.

This approach relies on the fact that the server the serves delegated
attributes would update change_attr on operations which might now
necessarily happen (ie, linux server does not update change_attribute
on writes or clone). I propose an alternative fix for the failing
generic/728.

diff --git a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
index 7b3ca68fb4bb..ede1835a45b3 100644
--- a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
+++ b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
@@ -1389,7 +1389,13 @@ static int _nfs42_proc_removexattr(struct inode
*inode, const char *name)
            &res.seq_res, 1);
        trace_nfs4_removexattr(inode, name, ret);
        if (!ret)
-               nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
+               if (nfs_have_delegated_attributes(inode)) {
+                       nfs_update_delegated_mtime(inode);
+                       spin_lock(&inode->i_lock);
+                       nfs_set_cache_invalid(inode, NFS_INO_INVALID_BLOCKS);
+                       spin_unlock(&inode->i_lock);
+               } else
+                       nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);

        return ret;
 }

> What's the advantage of doing it this way?
>
> You just sent a REMOVEXATTR operation to the server that will change

In presence of delegated attributes, Is the server required to update
its mtime/ctime on an operation? As I mentioned, the linux server does
not update its ctime/mtime for WRITE, CLONE, COPY. Is possible that
some implementations might be different and also do not update the
ctime/mtime on REMOVEXATTR? Therefore I was suggesting that the patch
relies on the fact that it would receive an updated value. Of course
perhaps all implementations are done the same as the linux server and
my point is moot. I didn't see anything in the spec that clarifies
what the server supposed to do (and client rely on).

View on lore ↗
Signals: alternative fix, requested modification
Olga Kornievskaia Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Olga Kornievskaia pointed out that the patch relies on an assumption about server behavior in delegated attribute scenarios, specifically that the server updates ctime/mtime for REMOVEXATTR operations. However, she noted that some implementations (e.g., Hammerspace anvil) may not update ctime/mtime for these operations, and requested clarification of the NFSv4.2 specification regarding timestamp updates under delegation.

> >
> > That is a very good point.
> >
> > My interpretation was that delegated timestamps generally covered
> > writes, but SETATTR style operations that do anything beyond only
> > changing the mtime can't be cached.
> >
> > We probably need some delstid spec clarification: for what operations
> > is the server required to disable timestamp updates when a write
> > delegation is outstanding?
> >
> > In the case of nfsd, we disable timestamp updates for WRITE/COPY/CLONE
> > but not SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> >
> > How does the Hammerspace anvil behave? Does it disable c/mtime updates
> > for writes when there is an outstanding timestamp delegation like we're
> > doing in nfsd? If so, does it do the same for
> > SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR operations as well?
>
> Jeff,
>
> I think the right way to look at this is closer to how size is
> handled under delegation in RFC8881, rather than as a per-op rule.
>
> In our implementation, because we are acting as an MDS and data I/O
> goes to DSes, we already treat size as effectively delegated when
> a write layout is outstanding. The MDS does not maintain authoritative
> size locally in that case. We may refresh size/timestamps internally
> (e.g., on GETATTR by querying DSes), but we don’t treat that as
> overriding the delegated authority.
>
> For timestamps, our behavior is effectively the same model. When
> the client holds the relevant delegation, the server does not
> consider itself authoritative for ctime/mtime. If current values
> are needed, we can obtain them from the client (e.g., via CB_GETATTR),
> and the client must present the delegation stateid to demonstrate
> that authority. So the authority follows the delegation, not the
> specific operation.

What happens when the client holding the attribute delegation (it's
the authority) is doing the query? Is it the server's responsibility
to query the client before replying. Example, client sends a CLONE
operation which has a GETATTR attached. (1) is the server supposed to
issue a CB_GETATTR before replying to the compound? (2) is the client
not supposed to send any GETATTRs while holding an attribute
delegation? CLONE is a modifying operation but client hasn't done any
actual modifications to the opened file so a CB_GETATTR would return
that file hasn't been modified. Is the server then not going to
express that the file has indeed been modified when replying to
GETATTR?

> That said, I don’t think we’ve fully resolved the semantics for all
> metadata-style ops either. WRITE and SETATTR are clear in our model,
> but for things like CLONE/COPY/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR, we’ve likely
> been relying on assumptions rather than a fully consistent rule.
> I.e., CLONE and COPY we just pass through to the DS and we don't
> implement SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
>
> So the spec question, as I see it, is not whether REMOVEXATTR (or
> any particular op) should update ctime/mtime, but whether delegated
> timestamps are meant to follow the same attribute-authority model
> as delegated size in RFC8881. If so, then we expect that the server
> should query the client via CB_GETATTR to return updated ctime/mtime
> after such operations while the delegation is outstanding.
>
> Thanks,
> Tom
>
>
> >
> >
> >
> > > > It's certainly possible that the REMOVEXATTR is the only change that
> > > > occurred. With what I'm proposing, we don't even need to do a SETATTR
> > > > at all if nothing else changed. With your version, you would.
> > > >
> > > > > >

View on lore ↗
Signals: requested changes, clarification needed
Olga Kornievskaia Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Olga Kornievskaia pointed out that the patch relies on a server behavior assumption, specifically that it updates mtime/ctime on REMOVEXATTR operations in presence of delegated attributes. She noted that the Linux server does not update ctime/mtime for WRITE, CLONE, and COPY operations, and questioned whether other implementations might behave similarly. She suggested clarifying the delegation timestamp specification to determine when servers should disable timestamp updates during write delegations.
Show original comment 
On Fri, Mar 27, 2026 at 4:02 PM Thomas Haynes <loghyr@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> On Fri, Mar 27, 2026 at 03:36:12PM -0800, Olga Kornievskaia wrote:
> > On Fri, Mar 27, 2026 at 2:22 PM Thomas Haynes <loghyr@gmail.com> wrote:
> > >
> > > On Fri, Mar 27, 2026 at 12:59:54PM -0800, Jeff Layton wrote:
> > > > On Fri, 2026-03-27 at 12:20 -0400, Olga Kornievskaia wrote:
> > > > > On Fri, Mar 27, 2026 at 11:50 AM Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org> wrote:
> > > > > >
> > > > > > On Fri, 2026-03-27 at 11:11 -0400, Olga Kornievskaia wrote:
> > > > > > > On Tue, Mar 24, 2026 at 1:32 PM Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org> wrote:
> > > > > > > >
> > > > > > > > xfstest generic/728 fails with delegated timestamps. The client does a
> > > > > > > > removexattr and then a stat to test the ctime, which doesn't change. The
> > > > > > > > stat() doesn't trigger a GETATTR because of the delegated timestamps, so
> > > > > > > > it relies on the cached ctime, which is wrong.
> > > > > > > >
> > > > > > > > The setxattr compound has a trailing GETATTR, which ensures that its
> > > > > > > > ctime gets updated. Follow the same strategy with removexattr.
> > > > > > >
> > > > > > > This approach relies on the fact that the server the serves delegated
> > > > > > > attributes would update change_attr on operations which might now
> > > > > > > necessarily happen (ie, linux server does not update change_attribute
> > > > > > > on writes or clone). I propose an alternative fix for the failing
> > > > > > > generic/728.
> > > > > > >
> > > > > > > diff --git a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > index 7b3ca68fb4bb..ede1835a45b3 100644
> > > > > > > --- a/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > +++ b/fs/nfs/nfs42proc.c
> > > > > > > @@ -1389,7 +1389,13 @@ static int _nfs42_proc_removexattr(struct inode

> > > > > > >             &res.seq_res, 1);
> > > > > > >         trace_nfs4_removexattr(inode, name, ret);
> > > > > > >         if (!ret)
> > > > > > > -               nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> > > > > > > +               if (nfs_have_delegated_attributes(inode)) {
> > > > > > > +                       nfs_update_delegated_mtime(inode);
> > > > > > > +                       spin_lock(&inode->i_lock);
> > > > > > > +                       nfs_set_cache_invalid(inode, NFS_INO_INVALID_BLOCKS);
> > > > > > > +                       spin_unlock(&inode->i_lock);
> > > > > > > +               } else
> > > > > > > +                       nfs4_update_changeattr(inode, &res.cinfo, timestamp, 0);
> > > > > > >

> > > > > > >
> > > > > >
> > > > > > What's the advantage of doing it this way?
> > > > > >
> > > > > > You just sent a REMOVEXATTR operation to the server that will change
> > > > > > the mtime there. The server has the most up-to-date version of the
> > > > > > mtime and ctime at that point.
> > > > >
> > > > > In presence of delegated attributes, Is the server required to update
> > > > > its mtime/ctime on an operation? As I mentioned, the linux server does
> > > > > not update its ctime/mtime for WRITE, CLONE, COPY.
> > > > >
> > > > > Is possible that
> > > > > some implementations might be different and also do not update the
> > > > > ctime/mtime on REMOVEXATTR?
> > > > >
> > > > > Therefore I was suggesting that the patch
> > > > > relies on the fact that it would receive an updated value. Of course
> > > > > perhaps all implementations are done the same as the linux server and
> > > > > my point is moot. I didn't see anything in the spec that clarifies
> > > > > what the server supposed to do (and client rely on).
> > > > >
> > > >
> > > > (cc'ing Tom)
> > > >
> > > > That is a very good point.
> > > >
> > > > My interpretation was that delegated timestamps generally covered
> > > > writes, but SETATTR style operations that do anything beyond only
> > > > changing the mtime can't be cached.
> > > >
> > > > We probably need some delstid spec clarification: for what operations
> > > > is the server required to disable timestamp updates when a write
> > > > delegation is outstanding?
> > > >
> > > > In the case of nfsd, we disable timestamp updates for WRITE/COPY/CLONE
> > > > but not SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> > > >
> > > > How does the Hammerspace anvil behave? Does it disable c/mtime updates
> > > > for writes when there is an outstanding timestamp delegation like we're
> > > > doing in nfsd? If so, does it do the same for
> > > > SETATTR/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR operations as well?
> > >
> > > Jeff,
> > >
> > > I think the right way to look at this is closer to how size is
> > > handled under delegation in RFC8881, rather than as a per-op rule.
> > >
> > > In our implementation, because we are acting as an MDS and data I/O
> > > goes to DSes, we already treat size as effectively delegated when
> > > a write layout is outstanding. The MDS does not maintain authoritative
> > > size locally in that case. We may refresh size/timestamps internally
> > > (e.g., on GETATTR by querying DSes), but we don’t treat that as
> > > overriding the delegated authority.
> > >
> > > For timestamps, our behavior is effectively the same model. When
> > > the client holds the relevant delegation, the server does not
> > > consider itself authoritative for ctime/mtime. If current values
> > > are needed, we can obtain them from the client (e.g., via CB_GETATTR),
> > > and the client must present the delegation stateid to demonstrate
> > > that authority. So the authority follows the delegation, not the
> > > specific operation.
> >
> > What happens when the client holding the attribute delegation (it's
> > the authority) is doing the query? Is it the server's responsibility
> > to query the client before replying.
>
> The Hammerspace server on a getattr checks to see if there is
> a delegated stateid (and whether it is the client making the request
> or not). If it is and the GETATTR asks for FATTR4_SIZE, FATTR4_TIME_MODIFY,
> or FATTR4_TIME_METADATA, then it will send a CB_GETATTR before
> responding to the client.

So... while I might not be running the latest Hammerspace bits and
something has changed but I do not see Hammerspace server sending a
CB_GETATTR when the "client is making a getattr request with a
delegated stateid". Example is the CLONE operation with an accompanied
GETATTR. The server in this case updates the change attribute and
mtime and returns different from what the client had previously in the
OPEN back to the client (this is what the test expect but I think
that's contradictory to what is said above). (To contrast nfsd server
does not update the change attribute or mtime and returns the same
value and thus making xfstest fail). So if the spec mandates that
before answering the GETATTR a CB_GETATTR needs to be sent then
neither of the server (Hammerspace nor linux) do that. But then again
I'm not sure the client is not at fault for sending a GETATTR in the
CLONE compound in the first place. For instance client does not have a
GETATTR in the COPY compound (and then fails to pass xfstest that
checks for modifies ctime/mtime). But the solution isn't clear to is
it like Jeff's REMOVEXATTR approach to add the GETATTR to the COPY
compound but that then should trigger a CB_GETATTR back to the client.
Again probably none of the servers do that...

Another point I would like to raise is: doesn't it seem
counter-intuitive to be in a situation where we have a
delegation-holding client sending a GETATTR and then a server needing
to do a CB_GETTATTR to the said client to fulfill the request.
Delegations were supposed to reduce traffic and now we are introducing
additional traffic instead. I guess I'm arguing that the client is
broken to ever query for change_attr, mtime if it's holding the
delegation. Yet the linux client (I could be wrong here) is written
such that change_attr or mtime has to always come from the server. Say
the application did a write() followed by a stat(). Client can't
satisfy stat() without reaching out to the server. Yet the server is
not the authority and before it can generate the reply for
change_attr, mtime, it needs to callback to the client (CB_GETATTR).
It seems it would have been better to never get a delegated attribute
delegation in the first place?

> While it does not do so, if the client sent in a SETATTR in the
> same compound we could short circuit that. Think a sort of WCC.
>
> > Example, client sends a CLONE
> > operation which has a GETATTR attached. (1) is the server supposed to
> > issue a CB_GETATTR before replying to the compound? (2) is the client
> > not supposed to send any GETATTRs while holding an attribute
> > delegation? CLONE is a modifying operation but client hasn't done any
> > actual modifications to the opened file so a CB_GETATTR would return
> > that file hasn't been modified. Is the server then not going to
> > express that the file has indeed been modified when replying to
> > GETATTR?
>
> The server could argue that the client wants to know what it thinks.
> But that isn't the argeement. The server has to query those values
> before sending them on.
>
> >
> > > That said, I don’t think we’ve fully resolved the semantics for all
> > > metadata-style ops either. WRITE and SETATTR are clear in our model,
> > > but for things like CLONE/COPY/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR, we’ve likely
> > > been relying on assumptions rather than a fully consistent rule.
> > > I.e., CLONE and COPY we just pass through to the DS and we don't
> > > implement SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> > >
> > > So the spec question, as I see it, is not whether REMOVEXATTR (or
> > > any particular op) should update ctime/mtime, but whether delegated
> > > timestamps are meant to follow the same attribute-authority model
> > > as delegated size in RFC8881. If so, then we expect that the server
> > > should query the client via CB_GETATTR to return updated ctime/mtime
> > > after such operations while the delegation is outstanding.
> > >
> > > Thanks,
> > > Tom
> > >
> > >
> > > >
> > > >
> > > >
> > > > > > It's certainly possible that the REMOVEXATTR is the only change that
> > > > > > occurred. With what I'm proposing, we don't even need to do a SETATTR
> > > > > > at all if nothing else changed. With your version, you would.

Signals: requested changes, clarification needed
Olga Kornievskaia Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Olga Kornievskaia noted that delegated timestamps may not be updated by all servers on operations like WRITE, CLONE, and COPY, and questioned the assumption in the patch that they would receive an updated value. She suggested that the patch relies on a server behavior that is not clearly specified in the protocol, and proposed clarifying the delstid spec to define when timestamp updates should be disabled for writes with outstanding delegations.

> > > > > Jeff,
> > > > >
> > > > > I think the right way to look at this is closer to how size is
> > > > > handled under delegation in RFC8881, rather than as a per-op rule.
> > > > >
> > > > > In our implementation, because we are acting as an MDS and data I/O
> > > > > goes to DSes, we already treat size as effectively delegated when
> > > > > a write layout is outstanding. The MDS does not maintain authoritative
> > > > > size locally in that case. We may refresh size/timestamps internally
> > > > > (e.g., on GETATTR by querying DSes), but we don’t treat that as
> > > > > overriding the delegated authority.
> > > > >
> > > > > For timestamps, our behavior is effectively the same model. When
> > > > > the client holds the relevant delegation, the server does not
> > > > > consider itself authoritative for ctime/mtime. If current values
> > > > > are needed, we can obtain them from the client (e.g., via CB_GETATTR),
> > > > > and the client must present the delegation stateid to demonstrate
> > > > > that authority. So the authority follows the delegation, not the
> > > > > specific operation.
> > > >
> > > > What happens when the client holding the attribute delegation (it's
> > > > the authority) is doing the query? Is it the server's responsibility
> > > > to query the client before replying.
> > >
> > > The Hammerspace server on a getattr checks to see if there is
> > > a delegated stateid (and whether it is the client making the request
> > > or not). If it is and the GETATTR asks for FATTR4_SIZE, FATTR4_TIME_MODIFY,
> > > or FATTR4_TIME_METADATA, then it will send a CB_GETATTR before
> > > responding to the client.
> >
> > So... while I might not be running the latest Hammerspace bits and
> > something has changed but I do not see Hammerspace server sending a
> > CB_GETATTR when the "client is making a getattr request with a
> > delegated stateid".
>
> Or the process is broken on the server side.
>
>
> > Example is the CLONE operation with an accompanied
> > GETATTR. The server in this case updates the change attribute and
> > mtime and returns different from what the client had previously in the
> > OPEN back to the client (this is what the test expect but I think
> > that's contradictory to what is said above). (To contrast nfsd server
> > does not update the change attribute or mtime and returns the same
> > value and thus making xfstest fail). So if the spec mandates that
> > before answering the GETATTR a CB_GETATTR needs to be sent then
> > neither of the server (Hammerspace nor linux) do that. But then again
> > I'm not sure the client is not at fault for sending a GETATTR in the
> > CLONE compound in the first place. For instance client does not have a
> > GETATTR in the COPY compound (and then fails to pass xfstest that
> > checks for modifies ctime/mtime). But the solution isn't clear to is
> > it like Jeff's REMOVEXATTR approach to add the GETATTR to the COPY
> > compound but that then should trigger a CB_GETATTR back to the client.
> > Again probably none of the servers do that...
> >
> > Another point I would like to raise is: doesn't it seem
> > counter-intuitive to be in a situation where we have a
> > delegation-holding client sending a GETATTR and then a server needing
> > to do a CB_GETTATTR to the said client to fulfill the request.
> > Delegations were supposed to reduce traffic and now we are introducing
> > additional traffic instead. I guess I'm arguing that the client is
> > broken to ever query for change_attr, mtime if it's holding the
> > delegation. Yet the linux client (I could be wrong here) is written
> > such that change_attr or mtime has to always come from the server. Say
> > the application did a write() followed by a stat(). Client can't
> > satisfy stat() without reaching out to the server. Yet the server is
> > not the authority and before it can generate the reply for
> > change_attr, mtime, it needs to callback to the client (CB_GETATTR).
> > It seems it would have been better to never get a delegated attribute
> > delegation in the first place?
>
> Or the client needs to change its behaviour in this scenario? Why ask
> as question to a source which is not the authority?

well i think it's because it's not able to generate the values needed
(ie the server is the only one that can generate the change_attr value
and mtime is also supposed to come from the server clock too)?

> >
> > > While it does not do so, if the client sent in a SETATTR in the
> > > same compound we could short circuit that. Think a sort of WCC.
> > >
> > > > Example, client sends a CLONE
> > > > operation which has a GETATTR attached. (1) is the server supposed to
> > > > issue a CB_GETATTR before replying to the compound? (2) is the client
> > > > not supposed to send any GETATTRs while holding an attribute

> > > > GETATTR?
> > >

> > > >
> > > > > That said, I don’t think we’ve fully resolved the semantics for all
> > > > > metadata-style ops either. WRITE and SETATTR are clear in our model,
> > > > > but for things like CLONE/COPY/SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR, we’ve likely
> > > > > been relying on assumptions rather than a fully consistent rule.
> > > > > I.e., CLONE and COPY we just pass through to the DS and we don't
> > > > > implement SETXATTR/REMOVEXATTR.
> > > > >
> > > > > So the spec question, as I see it, is not whether REMOVEXATTR (or
> > > > > any particular op) should update ctime/mtime, but whether delegated
> > > > > timestamps are meant to follow the same attribute-authority model
> > > > > as delegated size in RFC8881. If so, then we expect that the server
> > > > > should query the client via CB_GETATTR to return updated ctime/mtime
> > > > > after such operations while the delegation is outstanding.

Signals: requested changes, clarification needed
Olga Kornievskaia Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Olga Kornievskaia noted that the patch may cause timestamps to go 'backwards' when a server updates the c/mtime of a GETATTR in a compound with CLONE(COPY), and requested clarification on whether the patch should modify the behavior to fix issue 221 without breaking issue 407. She also pointed out confusion about the difference between time_access and time_modify values in SETATTR.
Show original comment 
On Tue, Apr 7, 2026 at 9:28 AM Olga Kornievskaia <aglo@umich.edu> wrote:
>
> Hi Jeff and all,
>
> With this patch and running against a server that would update the

> the CLONE) is setting a timestamp which is earlier than what's

> how to "show" it via test (I observe it on a network trace).
>
> This is seen testing either against Hammerspace which already updates
> the values or the linux server with the patch I'm working on to update
> timestamp for CLONE/COPY.
>
> I have to say I haven't figured out if this patch needs to modify such
> that it fixes 221 but doesn't break 407 or we are fundamentally

> holding an attribute delegation that needs to be solved differently.
> Like I said before, if the server were to update the value upon
> receiving the GETATTR in the compound (regardless of the origin),
